        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.5b10875"><meta property="og:title" content="Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/acsami.5b10875.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="A simple one-step thermal pyrolysis route has been developed to prepare carbon membrane from a natural leaf. The carbonized leaf membrane possesses anisotropic surfaces and internal hierarchical porosity, exhibiting a high specific capacity of 360 mAh/g and a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 74.8% as a binder-free, current-collector-free anode for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Moreover, large-area carbon membranes with low contact resistance are fabricated by simply stacking and carbonizing leaves, a promising strategy toward large-scale sodium-ion battery developments."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/acsami.5b10875.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931886671beb54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886671beb54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '44887430-6f51-4137-9bd2-dde3499d6f5d'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886671beb54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="931886671beb54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Carbonized-leaf Membrane with Anisotropic Surfaces for Sodium-ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b10875", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/medium/am-2015-10875t_0004.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b10875&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">A simple one-step thermal pyrolysis route has been developed to prepare carbon membrane from a natural leaf. The carbonized leaf membrane possesses anisotropic surfaces and internal hierarchical porosity, exhibiting a high specific capacity of 360 mAh/g and a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 74.8% as a binder-free, current-collector-free anode for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Moreover, large-area carbon membranes with low contact resistance are fabricated by simply stacking and carbonizing leaves, a promising strategy toward large-scale sodium-ion battery developments.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Initial+Coulombic+Efficiency&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_initial%20Coulombic%20efficiency">initial Coulombic efficiency</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the abundance of sodium sources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be a promising alternative for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), particularly for grid-scale energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 1 to 8">(1-8)</a> To achieve affordable and high efficiency SIBs, the development of high performance and cost-effective electrode materials is crucial. According to the well-developed LIB knowledge, great progress has been made in developing suitable SIB cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 9 to 14">(9-14)</a> However, the challenge for SIBs is the anode because graphite, which is the standard LIB anode, is very limited in its ability to store sodium ions because of the their larger ionic size. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Although exfoliating graphite and further functionalizing it (via doping or combining with other active materials) can greatly increase its performance in sodium-ion storage, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 16 to 20">(16-20)</a> the exfoliating process is time-consuming and results in much pollution. Therefore, a low-cost and easily fabricated pure-carbon electrode needs to be developed.</div><div class="NLM_p">Hard carbon, which is formed by short-range turbo static structures, has shown high capacity as an anode for SIBs in recent years. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 21 to 24">(21-24)</a> Biomass, which is abundant and renewable, provides a low-cost and environmental friendly hard-carbon precursor for SIB anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25-27)</a> There are several mechanisms for the storage of sodium ion in hard-carbon anode, for example, intercalation between short-range graphene layers, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28, 29)</a> nanopore filling, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30, 31)</a> and chemisorption on surface heteroatoms <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> or structure defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> In hard carbon, there are a lot of voids between the short-range turbo layers, which can provide many cavities for storing sodium ions, leading to a higher capacity compared to that of graphite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Several biomass sources, such as lignin <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> sucrose, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> peat moss, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> banana peels, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> cellulose nanofiber, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> silk, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40, 41)</a> and pomelo peels, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> have been used as carbon precursors, and their derived carbons have exhibited great performance as SIB anodes. However, these carbon powders need to be mixed with carbon black and polymer binders to form the electrodes using a slurry-coating method, which requires extra steps for battery fabrication and also results in an additional weight. To avoid the extra mixing process, simplify the battery fabrication process, and increase the energy density of the full battery, a free-standing carbon electrode is highly desirable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43-45)</a> Another problem of hard-carbon anodes is the low initial Coulombic efficiency (&lt;50%) caused by the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and irreversible Na<sup>+</sup> trapping in the graphene layers and defect sites, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> which results in an irreversible loss of sodium ions and a waste of cathode material in the full battery. Therefore, rational design of the pore structure in the carbon anodes for limited SEI formation and high capacity is crucial for high-performance SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Natural leaves, which are one of the most abundant biomass sources on Earth, with the main composition of cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> can be carbonized by thermal pyrolysis. As the anode for SIB, carbon derived from leaves outperforms that derived from other biomass sources by three points: (1) Leaves have anisotropic surfaces, in which stomata on the back surface (facing the soil) provide channels for the electrolyte to enter into the overlapping carbon nanosheets inside the carbonized leaf, and the nonporous upper surface (facing the sun) can work as a dense current collector, enabling the carbonized leaf to be a binder/current-collector-free anode for SIBs. (2) The pores in carbonized leaf are in the range of micrometers, resulting in a low surface area and limited SEI formation for higher initial Coulombic efficiency. (3) The carbon membrane from a leaf is free-standing with a thickness around 50â200 Î¼m, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 47 to 49">(47-49)</a> which is suitable for battery electrode application and avoids further mixing with other additives during battery fabrication process. Together with these properties, carbonized leaf could provide a high-performance and low-cost anode material for the scalable fabrication of SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here we prepared a free-standing carbon membrane from leaves, and for the first time, used it as a binder/current-collector-free anode for SIBs. The carbon membrane is highly conductive with a conductivity of 1259 sÂ·m<sup>â1</sup>. Hierarchical pores inherited from the leaf provide special channels for the electrolyte to access to the carbon nanosheets inside. Unlike nanoporous carbon powder and less-porous carbon bulk electrode, most of the pores in the leaf-based membrane are macropores, which are surrounded by amounts of nanosheets, thereby achieving a high capacity and high initial Coulombic efficiency simultaneously.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Materials Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Dry oak leaves were collected on campus and used without further treatment. The leaves were put into a tube furnace and carbonized at 1000 Â°C for 1 h under Ar flow with a ramping rate of 10 Â°C/min. To avoid curling, the leaf was fastened between graphite slides during the carbonization process. After the reaction, the carbonized leaf was immersed in 3.0 M HCl for 6 h to remove the inorganic impurities.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Characterization of the Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the carbonized leaf was observed with Hitachi SU-70 analytical scanning electron microscope (SEM) and a JEM 2100 FETEM transmission electron microscope (TEM). The structure of the carbonized leaf was measured with D8 Advance X-ray Diffraction (XRD) System and Horiba Yvon LabRam ARAMIS confocal Raman microscope with a heliumâneon 10 mW laser excitation source (632.8 Raman). BrunauerâEmmettâTeller (BET) analysis for nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms were performed on a Tristar II 3020 analyzer.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical properties of the carbonized leaf were determined using 2032 coin cells with Na plates as the counter/reference electrodes, and 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC)/ diethyl carbonate (DEC) as the electrolyte. The cells were tested by Arbin BT2000 battery tester.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the typical structure of a natural leaf with anisotropic surfaces, where the upper surface is flat, dense, and made up of many interconnected domains to prevent fast water evaporation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). In contrast to the upper surface, the back surface features many uniformly distributed stomata for gas exchange and water evaporation during transpiration (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). The inside of the leaf is highly porous with many cells to form the hollow tube structures. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d shows a cross section of the leaf, revealing the porous structure of its interior. Tubes beneath the upper layer are arranged vertically to form the palisade layer, which provides more chloroplasts for photosynthesis. Under the well-aligned tube array is the sponge layer, where the tubes are loosely and randomly distributed to leave enough air space for the exchange of oxygen and carbon dioxide during leaf respiration and photosynthesis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 50 to 53">(50-53)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/medium/am-2015-10875t_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of a leaf, and (b) its upper surface, (c) back surface, and (d) cross-sectional view.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b10875&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After carbonization at 1000 Â°C under Ar, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, the dried natural leaf turns black, indicating successful transformation into carbon. The carbonized leaf membrane maintains the shape of the original leaf, indicating good mechanical strength as a free-standing electrode for battery applications. In addition, the carbonized leaf membrane inherits the inner porous structure of the original leaf, as shown in the SEM image of the cross section in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. One can see the porous back surface and the dense upper surface clearly. The total thickness of the carbonized leaf is about 60 Î¼m, with the upper and the back surfaces being 5 and 2 Î¼m thick, respectively. The inner side of the carbonized leaf is highly porous with many nanosheets overlapped with each other, similar to the structure of freeze-dried graphene foam. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> This unique porous structure facilitates the transport of the electrolyte (blue arrows) with least resistance to the carbon sheets inside the carbonized leaf, entering through the porous back surface to the loosely arranged sponge layer (about 20 Î¼m) that has many voids located near the back surface, and finally to well-aligned palisade layer (about 30 Î¼m). Electrons travel along the conductive carbon sheet in the carbonized leaf and then are collected by the dense upper surface layer (red arrow). When zoomed in, it can be found that the thickness of the carbon sheet from the cell wall is in the range of 50â100 nm (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c), which allows for a short diffusion length for the sodium ions and leads to a high capacity. Because of the existence of inorganic elements in the natural leaves, there are some inorganic particles formed on the surface of the carbonized leaf (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/suppl_file/am5b10875_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1âS3</a>). X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/suppl_file/am5b10875_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) indicate that these particles are mainly Ca(OH)<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> which may result from the hydrolysis of CaO when the carbonized leaf is exposed to the moisture in air. These inorganic particles are easily removed by immersing the carbonized leaf in 3 M HCl for 6 h. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, the clean back surface exhibits a large number of grinning-mouth-like open macropores (stomata). These macropores are well-connected to those inside the leaf (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/suppl_file/am5b10875_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), facilitating the fast intrusion of the electrolyte into the inner pores of the carbonized leaf (blue arrows). From the upper surface, no pores are observed, but grains from quadrilateral to pentagon and then hexagon appear (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). These grains assemble like bricks into a flat dense surface, which is used as the current collector during electrochemical reactions (red arrow). The TEM image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f gives further insight into the microstructure of the carbonized leaf, where a typical morphology of amorphous carbon, having short-range aligned lattice and some voids were found. The measured lattice distance for the ordered region is 0.36 nm, slightly larger than that of graphite (0.34 nm).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/medium/am-2015-10875t_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0007.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Digital pictures of a dried leaf before and after carbonization. (b) SEM image of the cross-section of the carbonized leaf. (c) Magnified SEM image of the carbon nanosheet in the sponge layer. (d) SEM images of the stomata distributed uniformly on the back surface. (e) SEM image of the bricklike grains on the upper surface. (f) HRTEM image of the carbonized leaf.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b10875&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To study further the structure of the carbonized leaf, XRD pattern and Raman spectra were collected and the results are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. The XRD pattern in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows two broad diffraction peaks at 22.9 and 43.2Â° for (002) and (101) planes of the typical amorphous carbon. Raman spectrum of the carbonized leaf in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b exhibits two peaks for the D-band at 1346 cm<sup>â1</sup> and G band at 1598 cm<sup>â1</sup>, with an intensity ratio <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of 1.14. This further confirms the amorphous nature of the carbonized leaf, which can be used as anode for SIBs. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorptionâdesorption isotherm in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c demonstrates the pore structure of the carbonized leaf, which is a type II isotherm corresponding to the adsorption of the macroporous materials. The calculated surface area is 161 m<sup>2</sup>/g. This low surface area could induce limited SEI formation and thus a high initial Coulombic efficiency. The pore size distribution curve in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d shows limited amounts of nanopores. Most of the pores are macropores from the original leaf, which agrees well with the result of a low surface area.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/medium/am-2015-10875t_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD pattern and (b) Raman spectrum of the carbonized leaf. (c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorptionâdesorption isothermal curve and (d) the corresponding pore size distribution of the carbonized leaf.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b10875&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To study the sodium-ion storage properties, carbonized leaf is directly used as the electrode in coin cells without any binders, carbon additives, or current collectors. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the first five potential profiles of the discharge/charge process, where a long potential plateau occurs near 0 V, and a slope appears in the range of 0.2â1.2 V. According to previous studies, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> the sloping region corresponds to the insertion of sodium ion into the short-range domains of ordered graphic lattice, with a value of 215.0 mAh/g, whereas the capacity from the plateau of 264.2 mAh/g is related to the adsorption of sodium ions at the defect sites and nanovoids. Overall, the specific capacity is 358.6 mAh/g for the first charge process and 479.2 mAh/g for the first discharge process, resulting in an initial Coulombic efficiency as high as 74.8%. This value stabilized at about 100% from the second cycle on. Furthermore, the carbonized leaf maintains a high charge capacity of 360 mAh/g after five cycles at 10 mA/g. This excellent performance mainly results from the unique pore structure of the carbonized leaf, where the macropores are fully used but with limited SEI formation. The rate performance of the carbonized leaf is then studied, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. The specific capacity can reach 320 mAh/g when the current intensity increases to 20 mA/g and has a value of 270 mAh/g at 40 mA/g. Furthermore, the carbonized leaf shows stable cycling performance, which delivered high capacity retention over 200 cycles (90%, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). To study how the anisotropic surfaces affect the SIB performance, carbonized leaf was reversed as the electrode, where the upper surface faces to Na metal and back surface as the current collector. Interestingly, the first charge and discharge capacity dropped from 358.6 and 479.2 mAh/g to 246.2 and 325.1 mAh/g, respectively (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d), although the initial Coulombic efficiency remains roughly the same (74.8 to 75.7%). This reduced capacity may result from difficult sodium ion and electrolyte intrusion through the dense and thick upper surface layer.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/medium/am-2015-10875t_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of the carbonized leaf anode for SIBs. (a) First five charge/discharge curves. (b) Charge/discharge curves at the current densities of 10, 20, and 40 mA/g. (c) Capacity retention and Coulombic efficiency over 200 cycles. (d) Comparison of carbonized leaves with different configurations in charge and discharge capacities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b10875&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The thin and flat structure of the carbonized leaf is suitable for scalable fabrication of electrode for the SIBs. By simply stacking the leaves together before the carbonization process, a continuous carbon membrane can be obtained (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). These leaves are mostly welded together, as shown in the red box in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, where a continuous interface was found. From the magnified SEM image of a welded leaf (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c), no clear interface is observed between two carbonized leaves, indicating that most of the parts are indeed welded together. For the welding mechanism, several steps may be involved. First, organic components in leaves, such as cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin were decomposed, and small organic clusters were formed during the pyrolysis of leaves. Second, these clusters in the interface area between the two leaves would mix together to grow a uniform intermediate due to the moleculesâ diffusion. Lastly, the intermediate in the interface would be carbonized at high temperature, which led to the formation of welded carbon product. With this method, a 3 cm Ã 3 cm carbon membrane was fabricated (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). The conductivity of carbonized leaves was measured (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e). On the basis of the area of 1 cm Ã 1 cm and the thickness of 60 Î¼m, the obtained conductivity is 1259 sÂ·m<sup>â1</sup>. With the effective welding by carbonization as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, the conductivity of two leaves welded together by carbonization is 710 sÂ·m<sup>â1</sup> on the basis of the resistance measurement shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e. Note that thickness of the two leaves welded together is 120 Î¼m. We also compare the conductivity of welded leaves with that of carbonized leaves by physical contact (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/suppl_file/am5b10875_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). As the anode, the welded leaves delivered a specific capacity of around 210 mAh/g (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/suppl_file/am5b10875_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), which is lower than that of a single carbonized leaf for the longer ionic diffusion path. The membrane made of leaves welded together by carbonization with a high electrical conductivity and good electrochemical performance show great promise as a low-cost, scalable SIB anode.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/medium/am-2015-10875t_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Schematic of electrode membrane made of leaves welded together by carbonization. (b and c) SEM image of two leaves welded together by carbonization with an excellent contact, denoted by the red box. (d) A 3 cm Ã 3 cm electrodes made of carbonized leaves that are welded together. (e) <i>I</i>â<i>V</i> measurement of a carbonized leaf (original) and a stack of two leaves welded together by carbonization.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/asset/images/large/am-2015-10875t_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b10875&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">We have synthesized free-standing carbon membranes from abundant natural leaves by a simple one-step carbonization process. The unique pore structure of the carbonized leaf makes it an ideal candidate for the anode of SIBs, which shows a specific capacity of 360 mAh/g, a high initial Coulombic efficiency of nearly 75%, and a stable cycling life. Moreover, the thin and flat structure of the leaves makes it easy to fabricate a large-area carbon membrane with low contact resistance by simply stacking multiple leaves together before the carbonization process. The excellent electrochemical performance and easily scalable fabrication method of these carbonized leaves make them promising in large-scale stationary energy storage.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="NOTES-d4652e615-autogenerated"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.5b10875" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.5b10875</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of the back, upper, and cross sections of the as prepared carbonized leaf, XRD patterns of the as prepared carbonized leaf, resistance comparison of the leaves with different contacts, and electrochemical performance of  the welded leaves.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-temperature Stationary Sodium-ion Batteries for Large-scale Electric Energy Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ. Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2338</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2360</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c3ee40847g</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D2338-2360%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DH.%2BPan%26author%3DY.-S.%2BHu%26author%3DL.%2BChen%26title%3DRoom-temperature%2BStationary%2BSodium-ion%2BBatteries%2Bfor%2BLarge-scale%2BElectric%2BEnergy%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc3ee40847g&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.5b10875&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc3ee40847g&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2338-2360</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06467D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rapid consumption of nonrenewable resources resulted in an ever-increasing problem of CO2 emissions that has motivated people for studying the harvesting of energy from renewable alternatives (e.g. solar and wind).  Efficient electrochem. energy storage devices play a crucial role in storing harvested energies in the daily lives.  For example, rechargeable batteries can store energy generated by solar cells during the daytime and release it during night-time.  In particular, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have received considerable attention ever since their early commercialization in 1990s.  However, with initiatives by several governments to build large-scale energy grids to store energy for cities, problems such as the high cost and limited availability of Li starts to become major issues.  Na, which also belongs to Group 1 of the periodic table, has comparable electrochem. properties to Li, and more importantly it is considerably more accessible than Li.  Nonetheless, research into Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is currently still in its infancy compared to LIBs, although great leaps and bounds have been made recently in terms of research and development into this technol.  Here in this review, the authors summarize the recent advancements made, also covering the prospective materials for both the battery cathode and anode. The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium. In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Mechanisms of Lithium and Sodium Insertion in Carbon Materials</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report an inverse relationship between measurable porosity values and reversible capacity from sucrose-derived hard carbon as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  Materials with low measureable pore vols. and surface areas obtained by N2 sorption yield higher reversible capacities.  Conversely, increasing measurable porosity and sp. surface area leads to sharp decreases in reversible capacity.  Utilizing a low porosity material, we thus are able to obtain a reversible capacity of 335 mA-h/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  During the past decade humans have witnessed dramatic expansion of fundamental research as well as the commercialization in the area of electrochem. energy storage, which is driven by the urgent demand by portable electronic devices, elec. vehicles, transportation and storage of renewable energy for the power grid in the clean energy economy.  Li-secondary batteries and electrochem. capacitors can efficiently convert stored chem. energy into elec. energy, and are currently the rapid-growing rechargeable devices.  However, the characteristic (including energy d., cost, and safety issues, etc.) reported for these current rechargeable devices still cannot meet the requirements for elec. vehicles and grid energy storage, which are mainly caused by the limited properties of the key materials (e.g. anode, cathode, electrolyte, separator, and binder) employed by these devices.  Moreover, these key materials are normally far from renewable and sustainable.  Therefore great challenges and opportunities remain to be realized are to search green and low-cost materials with high performances.  A large no. of the properties of biomass materials-such as renewable, low-cost, earth-abundant, specific structures, mech. property and many others-are very attractive.  These properties endow that biomass could replace some key materials in electrochem. energy storage systems.  In this review, we focus on the fundamentals and applications of biomass-derived materials in electrochem. energy storage techniques.  Specifically, we summarize the recent advances of the utilization of various biomasses as separators, binders and electrode materials.  Finally, several perspectives related to the biomass-derived materials for electrochem. energy storages are proposed based on the reported progress and our own evaluation, aiming to provide some possible research directions in this field.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1021/nl400998t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jia, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Weadock, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Vaaland, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tin Anode for Sodium-ion Batteries Using Natural Wood Fiber as a Mechanical Buffer and Electrolyte Reservoir</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">13</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">3093</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">3100</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries offer an attractive option for low cost grid scale storage due to the abundance of Na.  Sn is touted as a high capacity anode for Na-ion batteries with a high theor. capacity of 847 mA-h/g, but it has several limitations such as large vol. expansion with cycling, slow kinetics, and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation.  An anode consisting of a Sn thin film deposited on a hierarchical wood fiber substrate simultaneously addresses all the challenges assocd. with Sn anodes.  The soft nature of wood fibers effectively releases the mech. stresses assocd. with the sodiation process, and the mesoporous structure functions as an electrolyte reservoir that allows for ion transport through the outer and inner surface of the fiber. A stable cycling performance of 400 cycles with an initial capacity of 339 mA-h/g is demonstrated; a significant improvement over other reported Sn nanostructures. The good Na-ion insertion property is attributed to the short diffusion distance in the hollow carbon nanowires and the large interlayer distance (0.37 nm) between the graphitic sheets, which agrees with the interlayered distance predicted by theor. calcns. to enable Na-ion insertion in carbon materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Murata, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ishikawa, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ozeki, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakayama, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ogata, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gotoh, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fujiwara, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Murata, Wataru; Ishikawa, Toru; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Nakayama, Tetsuri; Ogata, Atsushi; Gotoh, Kazuma; Fujiwara, Kazuya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA04995K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lotfabad, E.
