        </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of mech. milling on the microstructure, morphol. and electrochem. performance of graphite powders with respect to lithium insertion are studied.  After 150 h of ball-milling, the well-graphitized graphite has been pulverized into small particles with a size of about 50 nm, in which there are a lot of excess vacancies, microcavities and metastable carbon interstitial phases with sizes around 13 A.  Due to the large surface energy, the merging of single particles is favored and results in the formation of agglomerates with av. size about 1 Î¼m.  Voids are formed among the agglomerated particles.  The ball-milled graphite shows reversible specific capacity for lithium of 700 mA h g-1 (L1.88C6) with large hysteresis.  The large reversible capacity is due mainly to Li doping at vacancies, microcavities (or at the edges of the metastable carbon interstitial phase) and voids.  The bonding change between the interstitial carbon and the carbon in the arom. plane that is induced by insertion of Li atoms leads to hysteresis.  During charge-discharge cycles, the reversible capacity above 1 V decreases rapidly, which may be due to some vacancies and microcavities being annihilated by moveable and some bound interstitial carbon and to electrolyte penetrating gradually into voids formed by agglomerated particles during the Li insertion and desertion process.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1149/1.1870612"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Alcantara, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lavela, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ortiz, G.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are a promising candidate for grid electricity storage due to their potential low cost.  The development of anode materials is a crucial step to promote the commercialization of SIBs, and amorphous carbon materials are likely to be the most promising alternatives among all proposed anode materials.  However, the cost of the reported carbon materials is still very high due to the expensive precursors and their low carbon yield.  Here, we report an amorphous carbon (AC) material made from low cost pitch.  The amorphous carbon material with an amazing high carbon yield of 57% was achieved by utilizing the emulsification interaction between pitch and lignin to suppress the graphitization of pitch during the carbonization.  The effects of heat-treatment temps. and the pitch/lignin mass ratios on the morphol., microstructure and the electrochem. performance of AC were systematically investigated.  By optimizing exptl. conditions, we achieved one representative AC with a suitable morphol. and microstructure, which exhibits promising performances with a high reversible capacity of 254 mA h g-1, a high initial coulombic efficiency of 82% and excellent cycling stability.  This is the first demonstration that the pitch can be successfully applied in fabricating amorphous carbon anode materials for SIBs with superior low cost and high performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA05451B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are expected to be a promising com. alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale and low-cost elec. energy storage applications in the near future.  Despite this, the absence of a suitable neg. electrode material hinders their development.  In this contribution, the authors synthesized monodispersed hard carbon spherules (HCS) from an abundant biomass of sucrose, and studied the influence of the carbonization temp. on the microstructure and electrochem. performance.                         cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Nanosheet Frameworks Derived from Peat Moss as High Performance Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Wang, Huanlei; Li, Zhi; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Cui, Kai; Xu, Zhanwei; Zahiri, Beniamin; Tan, Xuehai; Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Olsen, Brian C.; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Peat moss, a wild plant that covers 3% of the earth's surface, serves as an ideal precursor to create Na ion battery (NIB) anodes with some of the most attractive electrochem. properties ever reported for carbonaceous materials.  By inheriting the unique cellular structure of peat moss leaves, the resultant materials are composed of 3-dimensional macroporous interconnected networks of C nanosheets (as thin as 60 nm).  The peat moss tissue is highly cross-linked, being rich in lignin and hemicellulose, suppressing the nucleation of equil. graphite even at 1100Â°.  Rather, the carbons form highly ordered pseudographitic arrays with substantially larger intergraphene spacing (0.388 nm) than graphite (c/2 = 0.3354 nm).  XRD anal. demonstrates that this allows for significant Na intercalation to occur even &lt;0.2 V vs. Na/Na+.  By also incorporating a mild (300Â°) air activation step, the authors introduce hierarchical micro- and mesoporosity that tremendously improves the high rate performance through facile electrolyte access and further reduced Na ion diffusion distances.  The optimized structures (carbonization at 1100Â° + activation) result in a stable cycling capacity of 298 mA-h g-1 (after 10 cycles, 50 mA g-1), with â¼150 mA-h g-1 of charge accumulating between 0.1 and 0.001 V with negligible voltage hysteresis in that region, nearly 100% cycling Coulombic efficiency, and superb cycling retention and high rate capacity (255 mA-h g-1 at the 210th cycle, stable capacity of 203 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1021/nn502045y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lotfabad, E.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Banana peel pseudographite (BPPG) offers superb dual functionality for Na ion battery (NIB) and Li ion battery (LIB) anodes.  The materials possess low surface areas (19-217 m2 g-1) and a relatively high electrode packing d. (0.75 g cm-3vs. â¼1 g cm-3 for graphite).  Tested against Na, BPPG delivers a gravimetric (and volumetric) capacity of 355 mA-h g-1 (by active material â¼700 mA-h cm-3, by electrode vol. â¼270 mA-h cm-3) after 10 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  A nearly flat â¼200 mA-h g-1 plateau that is &lt;0.1 V and a minimal charge/discharge voltage hysteresis make BPPG a direct electrochem. analog to graphite but with Na.  A charge capacity of 221 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 is degraded by 7% after 600 cycles, while a capacity of 336 mA-h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 is degraded by 11% after 300 cycles, in both cases with â¼100% cycling Coulombic efficiency.  For LIB applications BPPG offers a gravimetric (volumetric) capacity of 1090 mA-h g-1 (by material â¼2200 mA-h cm-3, by electrode â¼900 mA-h cm-3) at 50 mA g-1.  The reason that BPPG works so well for both NIBs and LIBs is that it uniquely contains 3 essential features: (a) dilated intergraphene spacing for Na intercalation at low voltages; (b) highly accessible near-surface nanopores for Li metal filling at low voltages; and (c) substantial defect content in the graphene planes for Li adsorption at higher voltages.  The &lt;0.1 V charge storage mechanism is fundamentally different for Na vs. for Li.  A combination of XRD and XPS demonstrates highly reversible Na intercalation rather than metal underpotential deposition.  By contrast, the same anal. proves metallic Li in the pores, with intercalation being much less pronounced.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref39" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta12389h"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Schardt, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Razink, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Simonsen, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Nanofibers Derived from Cellulose Nanofibers as a Long-life Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium-ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. The as-obtained HPNC-NS show favorable features for electrochem. energy storage such as high sp. surface area (SBET: 2494 m2/g), high vol. of hierarchical pores (2.28 cm3/g), nanosheet structures, rich N-doping (4.7%), and defects.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we have developed a new fabrication method for nanoparticle (NP) assemblies for Li-ion battery electrodes that require no addnl. support or conductive materials such as polymeric binders or carbon black. The NP film is formed by using electrophoretic deposition (EPD) of colloidally synthesized, monodisperse cobalt NPs that are transformed through the nanoscale Kirkendall effect into hollow Co3O4. Here, we present a free-standing TiO2 nanowire/graphene hybrid membrane for Li/dissolved polysulfide batteries with high capacity and long cycling life.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Attracted by the unique tissue and functions of leaves, a natural carbonized leaf (CL) is presented as a polysulfide diffusion inhibitor in lithium-sulfur (Li-S) batteries.  The CL that is covered on the pure sulfur cathode effectively suppresses the polysulfide shuttling mechanism and enables the use of pure sulfur as the cathode.  A low charge resistance and a high discharge capacity of 1320 mA h g-1 arise from the improved cell cond. due to the innately integral conductive carbon network of the CL.  The unique microstructure of CL leads to a high discharge/charge efficiency of &gt;98 %, low capacity fade of 0.18 % per cycle, and good long-term cyclability over 150 cycles.  The structural gradient and the micro/mesoporous adsorption sites of CL effectively intercept/trap the migrating polysulfides and facilitate their reutilization.  The green CL polysulfide diffusion inhibitor thus offers a viable approach for developing high-performance lithium-sulfur batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref49" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2006.04.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, S.                         cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced light-harvesting and photocatalytic properties in morph-TiO2 from green-leaf biotemplates</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xufan; Fan, Tongxiang; Zhou, Han; Chow, Suk-Kwun; Zhang, Wang; Zhang, Di; Guo, Qixin; Ogawa, Hiroshi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

In this work, green leaves are applied as biotemplates to synthesize morph-TiO2.  The structural features favorable for light harvesting from the macro- to the nanoscale are replicated in morph-TiO2 through a two-step infiltration process and the N contained in the original leaves is self-doped into the resulting samples.  The absorbance intensities within the visible-light range of morph-TiO2 derived from different leaves increase by 103-258% and the band-gap-absorption onsets at the edge of the UV and visible-light range show a red-shift of 25-100 nm compared to those in TiO2 without the template. The present work, as a new strategy, is of far-reaching significance in learning from nature, driving us to make full use of the most-abundant resources and structure-introduced functions endowed by nature, opening up possibilities for extensive study of the phys. and chem. properties of morph-structured oxides and extending their potential for use in applications such as solar cells, photocatalysts, photoelec. devices, and photoinduced sensors.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref52" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1038/srep01667"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fan, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ye, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Leaf-architectured 3D Hierarchical Artificial Photosynthetic System of Perovskite Titanates Towards CO<sub>2</sub> Photoreduction Into Hydrocarbon Fuels</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci.     <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1667, 9 pp.</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SRCEC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2045-2322</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of an "artificial photosynthetic system" (APS) having both the analogous important structural elements and reaction features of photosynthesis to achieve solar-driven water splitting and CO2 redn. is highly challenging.  Here, we demonstrate a design strategy for a promising 3D APS architecture as an efficient mass flow/light harvesting network relying on the morphol. replacement of a concept prototype-leaf's 3D architecture into perovskite titanates for CO2 photoredn. into hydrocarbon fuels (CO and CH4).  The process uses artificial sunlight as the energy source, water as an electron donor and CO2 as the carbon source, mimicking what real leaves do.  To our knowledge this is the first example utilizing biol. systems as "architecture-directing agents" for APS towards CO2 photoredn., which hints at a more general principle for APS architectures with a great variety of optimized biol. geometries.  This research would have great significance for the potential realization of global carbon neutral cycle.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201001626"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Schnepp, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Antonietti, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Giordano, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Biotemplating of Metal Carbide Microstructures: the Magnetic Leaf</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">49</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">6564</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">6566</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/anie.201001626</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D49%26publication_year%3D2010%26pages%3D6564-6566%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%252C%2BInt.%2BEd.%26author%3DZ.%2BSchnepp%26author%3DW.%2BYang%26author%3DM.%2BAntonietti%26author%3DC.%2BGiordano%26title%3DBiotemplating%2Bof%2BMetal%2BCarbide%2BMicrostructures%253A%2Bthe%2BMagnetic%2BLeaf%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.201001626&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.5b10875&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.201001626&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biotemplating of Metal Carbide Microstructures: The Magnetic Leaf</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schnepp, Zoe; Yang, Wen; Antonietti, Markus; Giordano, Cristina</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6564-6566</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have prepd. iron carbide replicas of a leaf skeleton.  By dispersing iron precursor in soln. form over the template, the microstructure of the leaf veins has been replicated with remarkable accuracy.  Significantly, the full structure of the leaf was intact after the heating procedure.  By demonstrating the use of these materials as electrodes for water splitting and electrodeposition of platinum, we showed that a homogeneous coating of Fe3C has been achieved over the whole structure.  The importance of magnetic leaf replicas lies not just in their intrinsic beauty and as proof-of-principle, but as a general procedure for biol. templating of metal carbide materials.  This method has great promise for the simple synthesis of a variety of microstructured objects for catalytic and electrochem. purposes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref54" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201204530"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gogotsi, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qiu, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultralight and Highly Compressible Graphene Aerogels</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultralight and Highly Compressible Graphene Aerogels</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Han; Zhao, Zongbin; Wan, Wubo; Gogotsi, Yury; Qiu, Jieshan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, we present ultralight chem. converted graphene aerogels with high compressibility.  In order to suppress the restacking of graphene sheets during, assembly, a strategy of functionalization-lyophilization-microwave treatment was designed.  Graphene oxides (GOs) are controllably functionalized and assembled into monolithic functionalized graphene hydrogel (FGH) in ethylenediamine (EDA) aq. soln.  Subsequent microwave irradn. (MWI) eliminates functional groups to produce ultralight graphene aerogel (ULGA) with excellent elasticity.  Its properties suggest applications for energy dissipation and vibration damping. The synthesis process of graphene aerogel with extremely low d. is discussed.  Firstly, a basic and weak reducing agent-EDA, was introduced into the GO colloidal soln. to functionalize it and initiate sheets assembly into a three-dimensional network.  The dispersion of GO first turned black, and then became sticky and finally transformed into hydrogel.  Importantly, the resultant hydrogel exhibited negligible vol. shrinkage after assembly, in contrast with the initial GO dispersion, which is remarkably different from graphene hydrogels prepd. via other methods.  After lyophilization, the solvent was removed from the FGH to produce functionalized graphene aerogel (FGA), which was subjected to MWI for making ULGA.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref55" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.4236/jsbs.2013.31010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bircan, S.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Production of hydroxylapatite from biowaste, chicken manure by hydrothermal process</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bircan, Sevgihan Yildiz; Naruse, Ichiro; Matsumoto, Kozo; Kitagawa, Kuniyuki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Sustainable Bioenergy Systems</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hydrothermal process has been applied for effective prodn. of Hydrogen from biowastes.  In this study hydrothermal process for prodn. of valuable Hydroxylapatite from chicken manure contg. phosphorus was focused on.  Conditions of 400 Â°C and 26 - 27 MPa with addn. of 1 mmol Ca(OH)2 were detd. as the optimal by using O-phospho-DL-serine as a model compd.  Afterwards, the real biowaste contg. phosphorous, chicken manure was processed under the same conditions. It was found that 27.9% of P in the chicken manure was converted to Hydroxylapatite.  With the use of acetic acid as a chem. purifn. medium, Hydroxylapatite was obtained.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886671beb54b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50'],'ref51':['cit51'],'ref52':['cit52'],'ref53':['cit53'],'ref54':['cit54'],'ref55':['cit55']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-temperature Stationary Sodium-ion Batteries for Large-scale Electric Energy Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06467D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rapid consumption of nonrenewable resources resulted in an ever-increasing problem of CO2 emissions that has motivated people for studying the harvesting of energy from renewable alternatives (e.g. solar and wind).  Efficient electrochem. energy storage devices play a crucial role in storing harvested energies in the daily lives.  For example, rechargeable batteries can store energy generated by solar cells during the daytime and release it during night-time.  In particular, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have received considerable attention ever since their early commercialization in 1990s.  However, with initiatives by several governments to build large-scale energy grids to store energy for cities, problems such as the high cost and limited availability of Li starts to become major issues.  Na, which also belongs to Group 1 of the periodic table, has comparable electrochem. properties to Li, and more importantly it is considerably more accessible than Li.  Nonetheless, research into Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is currently still in its infancy compared to LIBs, although great leaps and bounds have been made recently in terms of research and development into this technol.  Here in this review, the authors summarize the recent advancements made, also covering the prospective materials for both the battery cathode and anode.  Addnl., opinions on possible solns. through correlating trends in recent papers will be suggested.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.     </div><div class="casAbstract">New types of energy storage are needed in conjunction with the deployment of renewable energy sources and their integration with the electrical grid. Here we report a newly developed manganese hexacyanomanganate open-framework anode that has the same crystal structure.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1021/nl501152f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Mesoporous Amorphous FePO<sub>4</sub> Nanospheres as High-performance Cathode Material for Sodium-ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">14</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">3539</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">3543</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5343-5364</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Mechanisms of Lithium and Sodium Insertion in Carbon Materials</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.         </div><div class="casAbstract">VO2 (B) is an interesting cathode candidate in lithium-ion batteries with a high theor. capacity and fast charge/mass transfer rate. Reversible capacities of 400 mA h g-1 over 400 cycles (vs. Li/Li+) and 200 mA h g-1 over 200 cycles (vs. Na/Na+) have been achieved, indicating it is potential as an anode candidate either in lithium- or sodium-ion batteries.  Nevertheless, the reaction mechanisms seem to be different in Li and Na cells.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  During the past decade humans have witnessed dramatic expansion of fundamental research as well as the commercialization in the area of electrochem. energy storage, which is driven by the urgent demand by portable electronic devices, elec. vehicles, transportation and storage of renewable energy for the power grid in the clean energy economy.  Li-secondary batteries and electrochem. capacitors can efficiently convert stored chem. energy into elec. energy, and are currently the rapid-growing rechargeable devices.  However, the characteristic (including energy d., cost, and safety issues, etc.) reported for these current rechargeable devices still cannot meet the requirements for elec. vehicles and grid energy storage, which are mainly caused by the limited properties of the key materials (e.g. anode, cathode, electrolyte, separator, and binder) employed by these devices.  Moreover, these key materials are normally far from renewable and sustainable.  Therefore great challenges and opportunities remain to be realized are to search green and low-cost materials with high performances.  A large no. of the properties of biomass materials-such as renewable, low-cost, earth-abundant, specific structures, mech. property and many others-are very attractive.  These properties endow that biomass could replace some key materials in electrochem. energy storage systems.  In this review, we focus on the fundamentals and applications of biomass-derived materials in electrochem. energy storage techniques.  Specifically, we summarize the recent advances of the utilization of various biomasses as separators, binders and electrode materials. Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA04995K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lotfabad, E.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of mech. milling on the microstructure, morphol. and electrochem. performance of graphite powders with respect to lithium insertion are studied.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Banana peel pseudographite (BPPG) offers superb dual functionality for Na ion battery (NIB) and Li ion battery (LIB) anodes.  The materials possess low surface areas (19-217 m2 g-1) and a relatively high electrode packing d. (0.75 g cm-3vs. â¼1 g cm-3 for graphite).  Tested against Na, BPPG delivers a gravimetric (and volumetric) capacity of 355 mA-h g-1 (by active material â¼700 mA-h cm-3, by electrode vol. â¼270 mA-h cm-3) after 10 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  A nearly flat â¼200 mA-h g-1 plateau that is &lt;0.1 V and a minimal charge/discharge voltage hysteresis make BPPG a direct electrochem. analog to graphite but with Na.  A charge capacity of 221 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 is degraded by 7% after 600 cycles, while a capacity of 336 mA-h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 is degraded by 11% after 300 cycles, in both cases with â¼100% cycling Coulombic efficiency.  For LIB applications BPPG offers a gravimetric (volumetric) capacity of 1090 mA-h g-1 (by material â¼2200 mA-h cm-3, by electrode â¼900 mA-h cm-3) at 50 mA g-1.  The reason that BPPG works so well for both NIBs and LIBs is that it uniquely contains 3 essential features: (a) dilated intergraphene spacing for Na intercalation at low voltages; (b) highly accessible near-surface nanopores for Li metal filling at low voltages; and (c) substantial defect content in the graphene planes for Li adsorption at higher voltages.  The &lt;0.1 V charge storage mechanism is fundamentally different for Na vs. for Li.  A combination of XRD and XPS demonstrates highly reversible Na intercalation rather than metal underpotential deposition.  By contrast, the same anal. proves metallic Li in the pores, with intercalation being much less pronounced.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref39" class="rightTabRefNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit39" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta12389h"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Schardt, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Razink, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Simonsen, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon Nanofibers Derived from Cellulose Nanofibers as a Long-life Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium-ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The increasing demand for elec. vehicles and large-scale smart grids has aroused great interest in developing high energy d. storage devices. Here, we present a free-standing TiO2 nanowire/graphene hybrid membrane for Li/dissolved polysulfide batteries with high capacity and long cycling life. This hybrid electrode delivers a high specific capacity of 1327 mA h g-1 at 0.2 C rate, a Coulombic efficiency approaching 100%, high-rate performance of 850 mA h g-1 at 2 C rate, and long cyclic stability with a capacity of 1053 mA h g-1 at 0.2 C rate over 200 cycles, demonstrating great prospect for application in high energy Li-S batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b00112"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kushima, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Slurryless Li<sub>2</sub>S/Reduced Graphene Oxide Cathode Paper for High-performance Lithium Sulfur Battery</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">15</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1796</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1802</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biotemplating of Metal Carbide Microstructures: The Magnetic Leaf</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schnepp, Zoe; Yang, Wen; Antonietti, Markus; Giordano, Cristina</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6564-6566</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have prepd. iron carbide replicas of a leaf skeleton.  By dispersing iron precursor in soln. form over the template, the microstructure of the leaf veins has been replicated with remarkable accuracy.  Significantly, the full structure of the leaf was intact after the heating procedure.  By demonstrating the use of these materials as electrodes for water splitting and electrodeposition of platinum, we showed that a homogeneous coating of Fe3C has been achieved over the whole structure.  The importance of magnetic leaf replicas lies not just in their intrinsic beauty and as proof-of-principle, but as a general procedure for biol. templating of metal carbide materials.  This method has great promise for the simple synthesis of a variety of microstructured objects for catalytic and electrochem. purposes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201204530"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gogotsi, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qiu, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultralight and Highly Compressible Graphene Aerogels</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">25</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2219</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2223</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201204530</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultralight and Highly Compressible Graphene Aerogels</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Han; Zhao, Zongbin; Wan, Wubo; Gogotsi, Yury; Qiu, Jieshan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">25</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2219-2223</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, we present ultralight chem. converted graphene aerogels with high compressibility.  In order to suppress the restacking of graphene sheets during, assembly, a strategy of functionalization-lyophilization-microwave treatment was designed.  Graphene oxides (GOs) are controllably functionalized and assembled into monolithic functionalized graphene hydrogel (FGH) in ethylenediamine (EDA) aq. soln.  Subsequent microwave irradn. (MWI) eliminates functional groups to produce ultralight graphene aerogel (ULGA) with excellent elasticity.  Its properties suggest applications for energy dissipation and vibration damping. The synthesis process of graphene aerogel with extremely low d. is discussed.  Firstly, a basic and weak reducing agent-EDA, was introduced into the GO colloidal soln. to functionalize it and initiate sheets assembly into a three-dimensional network.  The dispersion of GO first turned black, and then became sticky and finally transformed into hydrogel.  Importantly, the resultant hydrogel exhibited negligible vol. shrinkage after assembly, in contrast with the initial GO dispersion, which is remarkably different from graphene hydrogels prepd. via other methods.  After lyophilization, the solvent was removed from the FGH to produce functionalized graphene aerogel (FGA), which was subjected to MWI for making ULGA.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.4236/jsbs.2013.31010"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bircan, S. Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Naruse, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Matsumoto, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kitagawa, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Production of Hydroxylapatite from Biowaste, Chicken Manure by Hydrothermal Process</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Sustainable Bioenergy Syst.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">3</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">74</span><span class="NLM_x">â</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">78</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.4236/jsbs.2013.31010</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Production of hydroxylapatite from biowaste, chicken manure by hydrothermal process</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bircan, Sevgihan Yildiz; Naruse, Ichiro; Matsumoto, Kozo; Kitagawa, Kuniyuki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Sustainable Bioenergy Systems</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">74-78</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JSBSBF</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2165-4018</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Scientific Research Publishing, Inc.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hydrothermal process has been applied for effective prodn. of Hydrogen from biowastes.  In this study hydrothermal process for prodn. of valuable Hydroxylapatite from chicken manure contg. phosphorus was focused on.  Conditions of 400 Â°C and 26 - 27 MPa with addn. of 1 mmol Ca(OH)2 were detd. as the optimal by using O-phospho-DL-serine as a model compd.  Afterwards, the real biowaste contg. phosphorous, chicken manure was processed under the same conditions.  Formation of a Hydroxylapatite; in the solid residue was confirmed from X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, after purifn.  It was found that 27.9% of P in the chicken manure was converted to Hydroxylapatite.  With the use of acetic acid as a chem. purifn. medium, Hydroxylapatite was obtained.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-NOTES-d4652e615-autogenerated"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i8">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.5b10875" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.5b10875</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of the back, upper, and cross sections of the as prepared carbonized leaf, XRD patterns of the as prepared carbonized leaf, resistance comparison of the leaves with different contacts, and electrochemical performance of  the welded leaves. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/suppl_file/am5b10875_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b10875/suppl_file/am5b10875_si_001.pdf">am5b10875_si_001.pdf (8.01 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="931886671beb54b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="931886671beb54b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage =
          "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);
          //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
          //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
          //     $(this).off();
          //   }
          // });
          $("#gdpr-con-btn").click(function () {
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            hideForm();
          });
        }, time);
      }

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    $(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
  </script>
</p>

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
